<!--Polyfills CustomEvent constructor in IE. Allows us to use events to manage race conditions in client side js-->
<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41588"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="The draft genome of tropical fruit durian ( Durio zibethinus )"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature Genetics 2017 49:11"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2017-10-09"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2017 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="The assembly of the durian genome provides insights into the unique flavor profile of this tropical fruit. Transcriptome and metabolome analyses show that methionine &#947;-lyase is upregulated and that volatile sulfur compounds are produced during ripening. Durian (Durio zibethinus) is a Southeast Asian tropical plant known for its hefty, spine-covered fruit and sulfury and onion-like odor. Here we present a draft genome assembly of D. zibethinus, representing the third plant genus in the Malvales order and first in the Helicteroideae subfamily to be sequenced. Single-molecule sequencing and chromosome contact maps enabled assembly of the highly heterozygous durian genome at chromosome-scale resolution. Transcriptomic analysis showed upregulation of sulfur-, ethylene-, and lipid-related pathways in durian fruits. We observed paleopolyploidization events shared by durian and cotton and durian-specific gene expansions in MGL (methionine &#947;-lyase), associated with production of volatile sulfur compounds (VSCs). MGL and the ethylene-related gene ACS (aminocyclopropane-1-carboxylic acid synthase) were upregulated in fruits concomitantly with their downstream metabolites (VSCs and ethylene), suggesting a potential association between ethylene biosynthesis and methionine regeneration via the Yang cycle. The durian genome provides a resource for tropical fruit biology and agronomy."/>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs2" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs2">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs2-content"><p>Durian (<i>Durio zibethinus</i>) is a Southeast Asian tropical plant known for its hefty, spine-covered fruit and sulfury and onion-like odor. Here we present a draft genome assembly of <i>D. zibethinus</i>, representing the third plant genus in the Malvales order and first in the Helicteroideae subfamily to be sequenced. Single-molecule sequencing and chromosome contact maps enabled assembly of the highly heterozygous durian genome at chromosome-scale resolution. Transcriptomic analysis showed upregulation of sulfur-, ethylene-, and lipid-related pathways in durian fruits. We observed paleopolyploidization events shared by durian and cotton and durian-specific gene expansions in <i>MGL</i> (methionine Î³-lyase), associated with production of volatile sulfur compounds (VSCs). <i>MGL</i> and the ethylene-related gene <i>ACS</i> (aminocyclopropane-1-carboxylic acid synthase) were upregulated in fruits concomitantly with their downstream metabolites (VSCs and ethylene), suggesting a potential association between ethylene biosynthesis and methionine regeneration via the Yang cycle. The durian genome provides a resource for tropical fruit biology and agronomy.</p></div></div></section>
                <noscript>
                    
                </noscript>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Main"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Main</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Durian (<i>D. zibethinus</i>) is an edible tropical fruit endemic to Southeast Asia. In the region, durian is known as the 'king of fruits' for its formidable spiny husk, overpowering flavor, and unique odor, described as an onion-like, sulfury aroma with notes of sweet fruitiness and savory soup seasoning<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Li, J.X., Schieberle, P. &amp; Steinhaus, M. Characterization of the major odor-active compounds in Thai durian (Durio zibethinus L. 'Monthong') by aroma extract dilution analysis and headspace gas chromatographyâolfactometry. J. Agric. Food Chem. 60, 11253â11262 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.3972#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d33465e757">1</a></sup>. Durian can elicit opposing opinions, from devotion to revulsion; the famed naturalist Alfred Russel Wallace once remarked on durian: âthe more you eat of it, the less you feel inclined to stopâ (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Wallace, A.R. On the bamboo and durian of Borneo. Hooker's. J. Bot. 8, 225â230 (1856)." href="/articles/ng.3972#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d33465e760">2</a>). In contrast, durian is banned from public transportation and many hotels because of its characteristic and pungent smell, described by some detractors as âturpentine and onions, garnished with a gym sockâ (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Winokur, J. The Traveling Curmudgeon: Irreverent Notes, Quotes, and Anecdotes on Dismal Destinations, Excess Baggage, the Full Upright Position, and Other Reasons Not to Go There (Sasquatch Books, 2003)." href="/articles/ng.3972#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d33465e763">3</a>).</p><p>Among the 30 known species in the <i>Durio</i> genus, <i>D. zibethinus</i> is the most prized as a major Southeast Asian food crop. The three leading durian-producing countries are Thailand, Malaysia, and Indonesia, with more than 250,000 ha cultivated in 2008 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Siriphanich, J. Postharvest Biology and Technology of Tropical and Subtropical Fruits (Woodhead Publishing Limited, 2011)." href="/articles/ng.3972#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d33465e775">4</a>). Durian is also of major economic value, as it has recently gained market penetrance in China: in 2016 alone, durian imports into China accounted for about $600 million, as compared to $200 million for oranges, one of China's other main fruit imports (UN Trade Statistics; see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="section anchor" href="/articles/ng.3972#Sec29">URLs</a>). More than 200 different cultivars of durian exist, encompassing a range of fruit textures, flavors, and aromas. Distinct regional demands for different cultivars reflect local idiosyncrasies in consumer tastes: pungent and bitter varieties are prized in Malaysia and Singapore (for example, Musang King), whereas sweeter cultivars with a mild odor are popular in Thailand (for example, Monthong). The distinctive odors of different durian cultivars have also been biochemically studied and characterized as a complex suite of odor-active compounds including sulfur volatiles, esters, alcohols, and acids<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Siriphanich, J. Postharvest Biology and Technology of Tropical and Subtropical Fruits (Woodhead Publishing Limited, 2011)." href="/articles/ng.3972#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d33465e782">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Jaswir, I., Che Man, Y.B., Selamat, J., Ahmad, F. &amp; Sugisawa, H. Retention of volatile components of durian fruit leather during processing and storage. J. Food Process. Preserv. 32, 740â750 (2008)." href="/articles/ng.3972#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d33465e785">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Chin, S.T. et al. Analysis of volatile compounds from Malaysian durians (Durio zibethinus) using headspace SPME coupled to fast GCâMS. J. Food Compos. Anal. 20, 31â44 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.3972#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d33465e788">6</a></sup>.</p><p>Despite the importance of durian as a tropical fruit crop, durian-related genetic research is almost nonexistent. Important resources such as genetic maps are not publicly available for durian, and no plant species in the Helicteroideae subfamily have had their genomes sequenced and assembled. Relatives whose genomes are available include only the more distantly related cash crops within the larger Malvaceae family: <i>Theobroma cacao</i> (cacao; used in chocolate) and members of the <i>Gossypium</i> genus (cotton). Besides <i>D. zibethinus</i>, several other <i>Durio</i> species can produce edible fruits (for example, <i>Durio graveolens</i> and <i>Durio testudinarum</i>), occupying specific ecological niches where they have relationships with specific pollinators, primarily fruit bats and birds<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Li, J.X., Schieberle, P. &amp; Steinhaus, M. Characterization of the major odor-active compounds in Thai durian (Durio zibethinus L. 'Monthong') by aroma extract dilution analysis and headspace gas chromatographyâolfactometry. J. Agric. Food Chem. 60, 11253â11262 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.3972#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d33465e814">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Bumrungsri, S., Sripaoraya, E., Chongsiri, T., Sridith, K. &amp; Racey, P.A. The pollination ecology of durian (Durio zibethinus, Bombacaceae) in southern Thailand. J. Trop. Ecol. 25, 85â92 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.3972#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d33465e817">7</a></sup>. However, many of the other <i>Durio</i> species are currently listed as vulnerable or endangered.</p><p>Here we report a draft whole-genome assembly of the <i>D. zibethinus</i> Musang King cultivar, employing a combination of sequencing technologies. We used the durian genome to assess phylogenetic relationships with other Malvaceae members and to compare transcriptome data between different plant organs (fruit aril, leaf, stem, and root) and between fruit arils from different cultivars. Genomic and transcriptomic analyses provided insights into the evolution and regulation of fruit-related processes in durian, including ripening, flavonoid production, and sulfur metabolism. The durian genome provides a valuable resource for biological research and agronomy of this tropical fruit.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Genome assembly</h3><p>We extracted DNA from a <i>D. zibethinus</i> Musang King durian fruit stalk, generating 18.3 million PacBio single-molecule long reads (average read length of 6.2 kb) corresponding to <span class="stix">â¼</span>153Ã coverage of the <span class="stix">â¼</span>738-Mb durian genome whose size was estimated by <i>k</i>-mer distribution analysis (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3972#Fig6">Supplementary Fig. 1</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3972#MOESM3">Supplementary Table 1</a>). We performed a PacBio-only assembly using an overlap-layout-consensus method implemented in FALCON<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Yumoto, T. Bird-pollination of three Durio species (Bombacaceae) in a tropical rainforest in Sarawak, Malaysia. Am. J. Bot. 87, 1181â1188 (2000)." href="/articles/ng.3972#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d33465e854">8</a></sup>; as the durian genome is highly heterozygous, possibly owing to frequent outcrossing during cultivation, we additionally phased the contigs using diploid-aware FALCON-Unzip<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Chin, C.-S. et al. Phased diploid genome assembly with single-molecule real-time sequencing. Nat. Methods 13, 1050â1054 (2016)." href="/articles/ng.3972#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d33465e859">9</a></sup>. The subsequent haplotig-merged assembly was polished using Arrow over three iterations. For details on validation of the genome assembly, see the <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3972#MOESM1">Supplementary Note</a>.</p><p>The durian assembly was further refined in two stages using chromosome contact maps. CHiCAGO (<i>in vitro</i> chromatin reconstitution of high-molecular-weight DNA) and Hi-C (<i>in vivo</i> fixation of chromosomes) libraries<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Lieberman-Aiden, E. et al. Comprehensive mapping of long-range interactions reveals folding principles of the human genome. Science 326, 289â293 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.3972#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d33465e875">10</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Putnam, N.H. et al. Chromosome-scale shotgun assembly using an in vitro method for long-range linkage. Genome Res. 26, 342â350 (2016)." href="/articles/ng.3972#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d33465e878">11</a></sup> (<span class="stix">â¼</span>280 million read pairs each) were used to improve scaffold N50 to 22.7 Mb, with the longest scaffold being 36.3 Mb (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3972#Fig1">Fig. 1a</a>; GC content of 32.5%). The final reference assembly comprised chromosome-scale pseudomolecules, with 30 pseudomolecules greater than 10 Mb in length and covering 95% of the 712-Mb assembly (the pseudomolecules are hereafter referred to as chromosomes, numbered according to size; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/ng.3972#Tab1">Table 1</a>). These figures correspond closely to previous estimates of the haploid chromosome number of durian (1<i>n</i> = 28, 2<i>n</i> = 56)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Mangenot, S. &amp; Mangenot, G. EnquÃªte sur les nombres chromosomiques dans une collection d'espÃ¨ces tropicales. Bulletin de la SociÃ©tÃ© Botanique de France 109, 411â447 (1962)." href="/articles/ng.3972#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d33465e895">12</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Characterization of the D. zibethinus genome."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 1: Characterization of the <i>D. zibethinus</i> genome.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.3972/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.3972/MediaObjects/41588_2017_Article_BFng3972_Fig1_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.3972/MediaObjects/41588_2017_Article_BFng3972_Fig1_HTML.jpg" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="1384"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) Circos plot of the multidimensional topography of the <i>D. zibethinus</i> genome (right), comprising 30 pseudomolecules that cover <span class="stix">â¼</span>95% of the assembly. Concentric circles, from outermost to innermost, show (1) gene density, (2) repeat element density, (3) GC content, and (4) syntenic regions with <i>T. cacao</i> (left), the closest sequenced relative in the Malvaceae family that did not undergo a recent WGD event. (<b>b</b>) Distribution of repeat classes in the durian genome. (<b>c</b>) Distribution of predicted genes among different high-level Gene Ontology (GO) biological process terms.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.3972/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Statistics for the <i>D. zibethinus</i> draft genome</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/ng.3972/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>We experimentally estimated the size of the durian genome to be about 800 Mb by flow cytometry, close to the size from our <i>k</i>-mer analysis (738 Mb; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3972#MOESM3">Supplementary Table 2</a>). <i>k</i>-mer distributions showed two distinct peaks, indicative of either a diploid genome or autotetraploid genome with low heterozygosity (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3972#Fig6">Supplementary Fig. 1</a>). Mapping of syntenic regions within the assembly showed extensive shuffling of syntenic regions rather than the one-to-one correspondence expected for an autotetraploid genome. SNP calling on the final assembly yielded a heterozygosity rate of 1.14%, supporting the estimate from <i>k</i>-mer analysis (1%) and consistent with durian being a highly heterozygous organism.</p><p>We identified and masked 54.8% of the assembly as repeat regions (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3972#Fig1">Fig. 1a</a>). LTR/<i>Gypsy</i> repeats were the most abundant, making up 26.2% of the genome, followed by LTR/<i>Copia</i> elements (3.2%; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3972#Fig1">Fig. 1b</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3972#MOESM3">Supplementary Table 3</a>). In comparison to other genomes in the Malvaceae family, LTR/<i>Gypsy</i> families of repeats appear to have expanded in <i>D. zibethinus</i> (26.2% of the genome), <i>Gossypium raimondii</i> (33.1%), and <i>Gossypium arboreum</i> (55.8%) as compared to <i>T. cacao</i> (9%). Conversely, LTR/<i>Copia</i> repeats appear to have contracted in <i>D. zibethinus</i> (3.2% of the genome) and <i>G. arboreum</i> (5.5%) as compared to <i>T. cacao</i> (7%) and <i>G. raimondii</i> (11.1%).</p><p>We annotated the remaining unmasked <i>D. zibethinus</i> genome using a comprehensive strategy combining evidence-based and <i>ab initio</i> gene prediction (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3972#Fig1">Fig. 1a</a>). Using the Maker pipeline<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Holt, C. &amp; Yandell, M. MAKER2: an annotation pipeline and genome-database management tool for second-generation genome projects. BMC Bioinformatics 12, 491 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.3972#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d33465e1196">13</a></sup>, we incorporated 151,593 protein sequences from four plant species and 43,129 transcripts assembled from <i>D. zibethinus</i> Musang King RNA-seq data. 45,335 gene models were identified, with an average coding-sequence length of 1.7 kb and an average of 5.8 exons per gene. The vast majority of gene predictions (42,747) were supported by homology to known proteins, existence of known functional domains, or the presence of expressed transcripts (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3972#Fig7">Supplementary Fig. 2</a>). Using Blast2GO<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Conesa, A. et al. Blast2GO: a universal tool for annotation, visualization and analysis in functional genomics research. Bioinformatics 21, 3674â3676 (2005)." href="/articles/ng.3972#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d33465e1207">14</a></sup>, we annotated 35,975 predicted gene models with Gene Ontology (GO) terms. The annotated gene models were involved in processes such as defense response, fruit development, and carbohydrate and lipid metabolism (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3972#Fig1">Fig. 1c</a>), which may be of interest in the study of genetic variation between domesticated cultivars.</p><p>To verify the sensitivity of our gene predictions and the completeness and proper haplotig merging of our assembly, we checked core gene statistics using BUSCO<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="SimÃ£o, F.A., Waterhouse, R.M., Ioannidis, P., Kriventseva, E.V. &amp; Zdobnov, E.M. BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics 31, 3210â3212 (2015)." href="/articles/ng.3972#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d33465e1217">15</a></sup>. Our gene predictions recovered 1,300 of the 1,440 (90.3%) highly conserved core proteins in the Embryophyta lineage, of which 68.1% were single-copy genes and 22.2% were duplicated. We checked whether this high duplication rate (22.2%) indicated unmerged haplotigs in our assembly, using coverage statistics from the Illumina short reads. Among these duplicated genes, 93.1% had mean read coverage within 1 s.d. of the mean read coverage for single-copy core genes, showing that these duplicated genes likely exist as independent and distinct copies in the genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3972#Fig8">Supplementary Fig. 3</a>). The high number of independent duplicate genes is suggestive of a recent whole-genome duplication (WGD) event in the durian lineage, similar to the scenarios in <i>G. raimondii</i> (11.5% duplicate genes) and <i>G. arboreum</i> (12.2%), both of which had recent WGDs, and distinct from the scenario in <i>T. cacao</i> (1.2%), which has not undergone a recent WGD. Visualization of syntenic blocks between durian and cacao confirmed that chromosome-level blocks in cacao are duplicated across multiple durian chromosomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3972#Fig1">Fig. 1a</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Comparative phylogenomics shows durian paleopolyploidy</h3><p>To investigate the evolution of distinct durian traits, we compared the durian genome to ten other sequenced plant species. These included three plants in the same Malvales order (<i>G. raimondii</i>, <i>G. arboreum</i>, and <i>T. cacao</i>), six plants in the same Eudicots clade (<i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i>, <i>Populus trichocarpa</i> (poplar), <i>Glycine max</i> (soybean), <i>Vitis vinifera</i> (grape), <i>Coffea canephora</i> (coffee), and <i>Carica papaya</i> (papaya)), and <i>Oryza sativa japonica</i> (rice) as an outgroup.</p><p>Gene family clustering with OrthoMCL<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Li, L., Stoeckert, C.J. Jr. &amp; Roos, D.S. OrthoMCL: identification of ortholog groups for eukaryotic genomes. Genome Res. 13, 2178â2189 (2003)." href="/articles/ng.3972#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d33465e1279">16</a></sup> identified 32,159 gene families consisting of 327,136 genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3972#Fig2">Fig. 2a</a>; for clarity, only durian, <i>T. cacao</i>, <i>G. arboreum</i>, <i>G. raimondii</i>, and <i>A. thaliana</i> gene families are shown). 607 gene families, consisting of 1,764 genes, were unique to durian (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3972#MOESM3">Supplementary Table 4</a>). Durian shared the most gene families with the other Malvales plants (cotton and cacao), consistent with the placement of these three species in the same taxonomic order. Durian, cotton, and cacao also had similar numbers of gene families as <i>Arabidopsis</i> (within 99% of each other), further validating the accuracy and completeness of our gene predictions at the gene family level.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Comparative genomic analysis of durian with other plants."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 2: Comparative genomic analysis of durian with other plants.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.3972/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.3972/MediaObjects/41588_2017_Article_BFng3972_Fig2_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.3972/MediaObjects/41588_2017_Article_BFng3972_Fig2_HTML.jpg" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="474"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) Sharing of gene families by durian and three other Malvales plants, with <i>A. thaliana</i> as an outgroup. The number in parentheses indicates durian-specific gene families among all 11 plants considered. (<b>b</b>) Inferred phylogenetic tree across 11 plant species. Established WGD events are placed according to Vanneste <i>et al</i>.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Vanneste, K., Baele, G., Maere, S. &amp; Van de Peer, Y. Analysis of 41 plant genomes supports a wave of successful genome duplications in association with the CretaceousâPaleogene boundary. Genome Res. 24, 1334â1347 (2014)." href="/articles/ng.3972#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d33465e1329">24</a></sup>. Posterior probabilities for all branches are &gt;0.9999. (<b>c</b>) Frequency distributions of synonymous substitution rates (<i>K</i><sub>s</sub>) between collinear genes in syntenic blocks in durian and <i>G. raimondii</i>. Peak boundaries corresponding to recent WGDs (green shading) were derived from Gaussian mixture modeling. (<b>d</b>) Inferred phylogenetic tree of paralogous durian and <i>G. raimondii</i> genes originating from their respective WGDs. Numbers represent branching posterior probabilities. The tree topology shows that the divergence of durian and cotton paralogs (derived from a WGD) predated the divergence of durianâcotton orthologs (derived from speciation) with high branching posterior probability, suggesting a shared WGD occurring before durianâcotton speciation (60â65 million years ago; green circle).</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.3972/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>We derived 47 single-copy orthologous genes among the 11 plant species for phylogenetic analysis, taking the intersection of 344 single-copy gene families found by OrthoMCL and 395 single-copy gene families present across diverse plant species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Duarte, J.M. et al. Identification of shared single copy nuclear genes in Arabidopsis, Populus, Vitis and Oryza and their phylogenetic utility across various taxonomic levels. BMC Evol. Biol. 10, 61 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.3972#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d33465e1361">17</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3972#MOESM3">Supplementary Table 5</a>). We used BEAST2 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Bouckaert, R. et al. BEAST 2: a software platform for Bayesian evolutionary analysis. PLoS Comput. Biol. 10, e1003537 (2014)." href="/articles/ng.3972#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d33465e1367">18</a>) to generate and date phylogenetic trees on the basis of Bayesian analysis. Consistent with the phylogenetic ordering of durian, cotton, and cacao first proposed by Alverson <i>et al</i>.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Alverson, W.S., Whitlock, B.A., Nyffeler, R., Bayer, C. &amp; Baum, D.A. Phylogeny of the core Malvales: evidence from ndhF sequence data. Am. J. Bot. 86, 1474â1486 (1999)." href="/articles/ng.3972#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d33465e1374">19</a></sup>, our results suggest that cacao first diverged from the shared durianâcotton lineage 62â85 million years ago (95% highest posterior density (HPD) interval), followed by the divergence of durian and cotton 60â77 million years ago (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3972#Fig2">Fig. 2b</a>). For the other plant species, phylogenetic placement and estimated speciation dates were mostly in broad consensus with other studies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="MagallÃ³n, S., GÃ³mez-Acevedo, S., SÃ¡nchez-Reyes, L.L. &amp; HernÃ¡ndez-HernÃ¡ndez, T. A metacalibrated time-tree documents the early rise of flowering plant phylogenetic diversity. New Phytol. 207, 437â453 (2015)." href="/articles/ng.3972#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d33465e1382">20</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Li, F. et al. Genome sequence of the cultivated cotton Gossypium arboreum. Nat. Genet. 46, 567â572 (2014)." href="/articles/ng.3972#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d33465e1385">21</a></sup>.</p><p>Ancient WGDs (also known as paleopolyploidization events) are widespread in plant lineages and represent a powerful evolutionary force for the development of novel gene functions and the emergence of new species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Otto, S.P. The evolutionary consequences of polyploidy. Cell 131, 452â462 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.3972#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d33465e1392">22</a></sup>. All core eudicots share an ancient WGD termed the Î³ event<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Jiao, Y. et al. A genome triplication associated with early diversification of the core eudicots. Genome Biol. 13, R3 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.3972#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d33465e1396">23</a></sup>, and, among the 11 plant species analyzed, at least eight additional ancient WGDs have previously been identified<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Vanneste, K., Baele, G., Maere, S. &amp; Van de Peer, Y. Analysis of 41 plant genomes supports a wave of successful genome duplications in association with the CretaceousâPaleogene boundary. Genome Res. 24, 1334â1347 (2014)." href="/articles/ng.3972#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d33465e1400">24</a></sup>. To investigate WGDs in the durian lineage, we identified syntenic regions across the durian, cotton, and cacao genomes, with each region consisting of at least five collinear homologous genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3972#MOESM3">Supplementary Table 6</a>). We found 60% of the cacao genome represented by syntenic regions in the durian genome. Of these syntenic regions in durian, 25% were present in one copy, 37% in two copies, 36% in three copies, and the remainder (2%) in four or more copies (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3972#Fig9">Supplementary Fig. 4</a>). The observation that 75% of the genomic regions syntenic between durian and cacao are present in multiple copies in durian strongly suggests that the durian lineage underwent a WGD after speciation from cacao. The <i>K</i><sub>s</sub> (synonymous substitution rate) distribution between syntenic durian genes also exhibited a peak characteristic of a recent WGD (<i>K</i><sub>s</sub> = 0.24; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3972#Fig2">Fig. 2c</a>).</p><p>A WGD has also previously been shown for the cotton lineage, after its speciation with cacao<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Wang, K. et al. The draft genome of a diploid cotton Gossypium raimondii. Nat. Genet. 44, 1098â1103 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.3972#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d33465e1426">25</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Paterson, A.H. et al. Repeated polyploidization of Gossypium genomes and the evolution of spinnable cotton fibres. Nature 492, 423â427 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.3972#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d33465e1429">26</a></sup>. We investigated whether the WGDs associated with cotton and durian reflect the same event or represent two distinct evolutionary events, by resolving the ordering and dates of the WGDs alongside the durianâcotton divergence event. We extracted paralogous pairs of durian and cotton genes arising from their respective WGDs, established orthologous relationships between them, and estimated their phylogenetic relationships and divergence times (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3972#MOESM3">Supplementary Table 7</a>). Our analysis showed that paralogs within cotton and durian (originating from the WGD) each predated their orthologs (originating from the durianâcotton divergence; posterior probability for the phylogenetic tree branches â¥0.9981; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3972#Fig2">Fig. 2d</a>). This provides evidence that durian and cotton share a WGD that likely occurred before their lineages diverged and places the known cotton-specific WGD also within the durian lineage.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Upregulated gene expression in sulfur and ripening</h3><p>To investigate biological processes associated with durian fruit traits, we sequenced expressed RNAs (RNA-seq) from different plant organs of the Musang King cultivar, including ripe fruit arils and stem, leaf, and roots. In addition, we sequenced RNA from ripe fruit arils of two other durian cultivars (Monthong and Puang Manee). We performed three independent comparative transcriptomic analyses: (i) comparison of durian transcriptomic data from fruit arils (Musang King, Monthong, and Puang Manee combined, three arils each) against root, stem, and leaf transcriptomic profiles (combined); (ii) comparison of durian fruit arils (Musang King, Monthong, and Puang Manee combined, three arils each) against fruits of other plant species combined, specifically <i>Musa acuminata</i> (banana; <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra?term=SRA259656">SRA259656</a>), <i>Mangifera indica</i> (mango; <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra?term=SRA289054">SRA289054</a>), <i>Persea americana</i> (avocado; <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra?term=SRA172282">SRA172282</a>), <i>Solanum lycopersicum</i> (tomato; <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra?term=SRA049915">SRA049915</a>), and <i>Vaccinium corymbosum</i> (blueberry; <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra?term=SRA145423">SRA145423</a>); and (iii) comparison of Musang King fruit arils (three arils) against Monthong and Puang Manee fruit arils combined (three arils each).</p><p>Gene set enrichment analysis (GSEA)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Subramanian, A. et al. Gene set enrichment analysis: a knowledge-based approach for interpreting genome-wide expression profiles. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 102, 15545â15550 (2005)." href="/articles/ng.3972#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d33465e1501">27</a></sup> showed that genes upregulated in durian fruits relative to non-fruit organs were associated with distinct cellular pathways, such as sulfur-, ripening-, and flavor-related processes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3972#Fig3">Fig. 3a</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3972#MOESM3">Supplementary Table 8</a>). Enrichment in gene sets related to sulfur comprised five clusters, of which acidâthiol ligase enzymes (containing a number of key enzymes in carbonâsulfur reactions and flavonoid production) and methionine metabolic pathways (of which <i>MGL</i> is a key member)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Joshi, V. &amp; Jander, G. Arabidopsis methionine Î³-lyase is regulated according to isoleucine biosynthesis needs but plays a subordinate role to threonine deaminase. Plant Physiol. 151, 367â378 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.3972#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d33465e1514">28</a></sup> contained the most significantly upregulated leading-edge genes. Ripening-related gene sets included genes regulated by the MADS-BOX transcription factor family<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Giovannoni, J.J. Fruit ripening mutants yield insights into ripening control. Curr. Opin. Plant Biol. 10, 283â289 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.3972#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d33465e1519">29</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Seymour, G., Poole, M., Manning, K. &amp; King, G.J. Genetics and epigenetics of fruit development and ripening. Curr. Opin. Plant Biol. 11, 58â63 (2008)." href="/articles/ng.3972#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d33465e1522">30</a></sup>, SEPALLATA transcription factor family, and ethylene-related genes such as <i>ACS</i> (aminocyclopropane-1-carboxylic acid synthase), a key ethylene-production enzyme involved in ripening<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Burg, S.P. &amp; Burg, E.A. Role of ethylene in fruit ripening. Plant Physiol. 37, 179â189 (1962)." href="/articles/ng.3972#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d33465e1529">31</a></sup>. Other upregulated processes related to taste and odor included triterpenoid metabolism, which has been associated with the intensely bitter taste of <i>Ganoderma lucidum</i> (Lingzhi)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Nishitoba, T., Sato, H., Kasai, T., Kawagishi, H. &amp; Sakamura, S. New bitter C27 and C30 terpenoids from the fungus Ganoderma lucidum (Reishi). Agric. Biol. Chem. 49, 5 (1984)." href="/articles/ng.3972#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d33465e1536">32</a></sup>, and lipid-related genes involved in the production of C<sub>6</sub> volatile compounds (hexanal and hexanol), which have been associated with the green odor profiles of apples, tomatoes, and bananas, as well as undesirable rancid odors when present at high levels<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Eskin, N.A., Grossman, S. &amp; Pinsky, A. Biochemistry of lipoxygenase in relation to food quality. CRC Crit. Rev. Food Sci. Nutr. 9, 1â40 (1977)." href="/articles/ng.3972#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d33465e1543">33</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Rowan, D.D., Allen, J.M., Fielder, S. &amp; Hunt, M.B. Biosynthesis of straight-chain ester volatiles in Red Delicious and Granny Smith apples using deuterium-labeled precursors. J. Agric. Food Chem. 47, 2553â2562 (1999)." href="/articles/ng.3972#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d33465e1546">34</a></sup>. Conversely, processes enriched in non-fruit organs included photosynthetic pathways and the regulation of nitrogen metabolism, likely required by roots and leaves to manufacture amino acids.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Transcriptome analysis identifies differentially expressed pathways in durian fruit."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 3: Transcriptome analysis identifies differentially expressed pathways in durian fruit.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.3972/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.3972/MediaObjects/41588_2017_Article_BFng3972_Fig3_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.3972/MediaObjects/41588_2017_Article_BFng3972_Fig3_HTML.jpg" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="537"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>â<b>c</b>) Differentially expressed pathways in durian fruit arils versus stem, root, and leaf (<b>a</b>), durian fruit arils versus fruits from five other plant species (banana, mango, avocado, tomato, and blueberry) (<b>b</b>), and fruit arils from the Musang King cultivar versus fruit arils from other cultivars (Monthong and Puang Manee) (<b>c</b>). Circle sizes correspond to the number of differentially expressed genes in enriched pathways genes in enriched pathways. The diagonal in each plot corresponds to a region where gene expression was not different between the two groups. Genes in the regions above this line correspond to gene sets that are prominently upregulated in durian or Musang King fruit.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.3972/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>To determine whether the fruit-enriched processes are specific to durian, we next compared the transcriptomes of durian fruits to those from the fruits of five other species. GSEA showed that sulfur-related pathways, lipid-oxidation pathways, and ripening pathways related to ethylene as well as protein degradation were upregulated in durian as compared to other fruits, whereas some flavor-related pathways were downregulated (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3972#Fig3">Fig. 3b</a>). These results suggest that both sulfur-related pathways and specific ripening processes related to ethylene could be highly regulated in durian fruits, even in comparison to other climacteric fruits. A prominent role for these pathways in durian is supported in the literature, as VSCs have been identified as important components in durian odor<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Jaswir, I., Che Man, Y.B., Selamat, J., Ahmad, F. &amp; Sugisawa, H. Retention of volatile components of durian fruit leather during processing and storage. J. Food Process. Preserv. 32, 740â750 (2008)." href="/articles/ng.3972#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d33465e1591">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Chin, S.T. et al. Analysis of volatile compounds from Malaysian durians (Durio zibethinus) using headspace SPME coupled to fast GCâMS. J. Food Compos. Anal. 20, 31â44 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.3972#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d33465e1594">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Ketsa, S. &amp; Daengkanit, T. Firmness and activities of polygalacturonase, pectinesterase, Î²-galactosidase and cellulase in ripening durian harvested at different stages of maturity. Sci. Hortic. (Amsterdam) 80, 181â188 (1999)." href="/articles/ng.3972#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d33465e1597">35</a></sup>. Ethylene production and cellular respiration are also increased in durian fruits during the climacteric stage of ripening and are associated with important physiological changes, including odor production, pulp softening, sugar release, and water loss followed by dehiscence<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Li, J.X., Schieberle, P. &amp; Steinhaus, M. Characterization of the major odor-active compounds in Thai durian (Durio zibethinus L. 'Monthong') by aroma extract dilution analysis and headspace gas chromatographyâolfactometry. J. Agric. Food Chem. 60, 11253â11262 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.3972#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d33465e1601">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Maninang, J.S., Wongs-Aree, C., Kanlayanarat, S., Sugaya, S. &amp; Gemma, H. Influence of maturity and postharvest treatment on the volatile profile and physiological properties of the durian (Durio zibethinus Murray) fruit. Int. Food Res. J. 18, 1067â1075 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.3972#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d33465e1604">36</a></sup>.</p><p>We also investigated whether differences in sulfur-, ripening-, and flavor-related pathways might be associated with the distinct and multifarious odor descriptors ascribed to different durian cultivars. GSEA results showed that all three pathways were significantly upregulated (sulfur metabolic process, <i>P</i> = 0.0019 by KolmogorovâSmirnov test; response to ethylene, <i>P</i> = 0.0009; flavonoid metabolic process, <i>P</i> = 0.0009) in Musang King as compared to Monthong and Puang Manee (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3972#Fig3">Fig. 3c</a>). These findings correlate well with the stronger perceived taste and smell of the Musang King cultivar.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Genomic expansions of volatile sulfur compound genes</h3><p>Durian aroma comprises at least two major groups of volatile compounds: (i) sulfur-containing volatiles, such as thiols, disulfides, and trisulfides, which may contribute to the distinct roasty, onion-like odor of durian, and (ii) esters, which may contribute to a sweeter, fruity odor<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Jaswir, I., Che Man, Y.B., Selamat, J., Ahmad, F. &amp; Sugisawa, H. Retention of volatile components of durian fruit leather during processing and storage. J. Food Process. Preserv. 32, 740â750 (2008)." href="/articles/ng.3972#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d33465e1631">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Chin, S.T. et al. Analysis of volatile compounds from Malaysian durians (Durio zibethinus) using headspace SPME coupled to fast GCâMS. J. Food Compos. Anal. 20, 31â44 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.3972#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d33465e1634">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Landaud, S., Helinck, S. &amp; Bonnarme, P. Formation of volatile sulfur compounds and metabolism of methionine and other sulfur compounds in fermented food. Appl. Microbiol. Biotechnol. 77, 1191â1205 (2008)." href="/articles/ng.3972#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d33465e1637">37</a></sup>. The former group (VSCs) is of particular interest, as its odor descriptors most closely reflect the distinctive nature of the durian aroma. Interestingly, analysis of the durian genome identified evolutionary gene family expansions of the <i>MGL</i> gene (four copies in durian, three copies in <i>G. arboreum</i>, two copies in <i>G. raimondii</i>, and one copy in <i>T. cacao</i>, <i>A. thaliana</i>, and <i>O. sativa</i>; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3972#Fig4">Fig. 4a</a>). Studies in microbes and plants have shown that <i>MGL</i> is a major functional contributor to VSC biosynthesis, degrading the sulfur-containing amino acids cysteine and methionine into methanethiol, which is further broken down into di- and trisulfides<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Li, J.X., Schieberle, P. &amp; Steinhaus, M. Characterization of the major odor-active compounds in Thai durian (Durio zibethinus L. 'Monthong') by aroma extract dilution analysis and headspace gas chromatographyâolfactometry. J. Agric. Food Chem. 60, 11253â11262 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.3972#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d33465e1666">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="RÃ©beillÃ©, F. et al. Methionine catabolism in Arabidopsis cells is initiated by a Î³-cleavage process and leads to S-methylcysteine and isoleucine syntheses. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 103, 15687â15692 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.3972#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d33465e1669">38</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Gonda, I. et al. Catabolism of l-methionine in the formation of sulfur and other volatiles in melon (Cucumis melo L.) fruit. Plant J. 74, 458â472 (2013)." href="/articles/ng.3972#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d33465e1672">39</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3972#Fig4">Fig. 4b</a>). Phylogenetic analysis of MGL proteins from durian and other species identified two evolutionary <i>MGL</i> subgroups (posterior probability &gt; 0.9999; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3972#Fig4">Fig. 4c</a>). The first group, referred to as <i>MGLa</i>, included singular <i>MGL</i> genes from <i>T. cacao</i> and both cotton species, as well as two durian <i>MGL</i> genes, <i>MGLa1</i> (Duzib1.0C006G000732) and <i>MGLa2</i> (Duzib1.0C011G000528). This group likely represents the ancestral, conserved <i>MGL</i> gene family. The second <i>MGL</i> group included only the two remaining duplicated copies of <i>MGL</i> from durian, suggesting that it comprises <i>MGL</i> genes that have diverged in sequence after genome duplication. We denoted the <i>MGL</i> genes in this second group <i>MGLb1</i> (Duzib1.0C010G000484) and <i>MGLb2</i> (Duzib1.0C010G000488).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Gene family analysis showing expansion in sulfur-related pathways associated with volatile sulfur compounds."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 4: Gene family analysis showing expansion in sulfur-related pathways associated with volatile sulfur compounds.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.3972/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.3972/MediaObjects/41588_2017_Article_BFng3972_Fig4_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.3972/MediaObjects/41588_2017_Article_BFng3972_Fig4_HTML.jpg" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="612"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) Gene families in sulfur, ethylene, and lipid metabolism pathways expanded in durian, including the <i>MGL</i> family. An asterisk indicates gene families expanded in both <i>Gossypium</i> and durian. (<b>b</b>) The VSC pathway, which breaks down cysteine and methionine into methanethiol and other VSCs, via the MGL enzyme. (<b>c</b>) Phylogeny of the <i>MGL</i> genes in durian, cotton, cacao, <i>Arabidopsis</i>, and rice showing two groups of durian <i>MGL</i> genes, denoted <i>MGLa</i> and <i>MGLb</i>. Numbers correspond to branching posterior probabilities. (<b>d</b>) <i>MGLb</i> durian genes are upregulated in durian fruit. MSK, Musang King; MT, Monthong; PM, Puang Manee. (<b>e</b>) The three genomic regions containing the durian <i>MGLa</i> and <i>MGLb</i> genes show clear synteny with the cacao genome, while the two <i>MGLb</i> genes occur in tandem. This suggests the involvement of both WGD and tandem duplication events in <i>MGL</i> family expansion.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.3972/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>To investigate the functions of the <i>MGLa</i> and <i>MGLb</i> genes in durian, we examined differences in their expression between durian fruit arils and non-fruit organs (stem, root, and leaf). <i>MGLb1</i> and <i>MGLb2</i> were consistently upregulated in fruits, with <i>MGLb2</i> in particular showing dramatically higher expression (DESeq2; 12.73- and 2,241-fold increase relative to non-fruit organs, <i>P</i> = 2.07 Ã 10<sup>â4</sup> and 9.26 Ã 10<sup>â100</sup> for <i>MGLb1</i> and <i>MGLb2</i>, respectively; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3972#Fig4">Fig. 4d</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3972#MOESM3">Supplementary Table 9</a>). In contrast, among the <i>MGLa</i> genes, <i>MGLa1</i> exhibited decreased expression in fruits (7.14-fold decrease, <i>P</i> = 4.44 Ã 10<sup>â3</sup>). These results suggest a novel function for durian <i>MGLb</i> in durian fruits.</p><p>To investigate potential mechanisms by which <i>MGL</i> might have expanded from a single copy in the durianâcacao ancestor to four copies in durian, we compared the <i>MGL</i>-containing genomic regions in cacao and durian. The two durian <i>MGLa</i> genes were present on two separate scaffolds, both of which showed regional synteny to the cacao <i>MGL</i> region. In contrast, the two durian <i>MGLb</i> genes were present in close proximity on the same scaffold, which also displayed synteny with the cacao <i>MGL</i> region (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3972#Fig4">Fig. 4e</a>). This suggests that <i>MGL</i> initially expanded in the durian lineage via a large-scale genomic duplication event (such as a WGD), followed by a subsequent tandem duplication event that doubled the durian <i>MGLb</i> genes.</p><p>Besides <i>MGL</i>, we also observed significant genomic expansions at the pathway level in gene families related to sulfur metabolism (false discovery rate (FDR) = 0.032), fatty acid metabolism (FDR = 0.018), and ethylene processes (FDR = 1.76 Ã 10<sup>â7</sup>) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3972#Fig4">Fig. 4a</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3972#MOESM3">Supplementary Table 10</a>). These included the <i>APS</i> (ATP sulfurylase) family, involved in hydrogen sulfide biosynthesis; <i>OASA1</i> (cysteine synthase), involved in maintaining sulfur levels; the <i>THIK</i> (3-ketoacyl-COA thiolase) family, involved in the production of lipid volatiles; <i>XBAT32</i> (an E3 ubiquitinâprotein ligase), a regulator of ethylene biosynthesis; and <i>ETR2</i> (ethylene receptor) and <i>AHK5</i> (histidine kinase), involved in ethylene signaling. Interestingly, some genes in these pathways were expanded in both durian and cotton, supporting findings of the importance of ethylene processing in cotton<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Li, F. et al. Genome sequence of the cultivated cotton Gossypium arboreum. Nat. Genet. 46, 567â572 (2014)." href="/articles/ng.3972#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d33465e1917">21</a></sup>. Taken together, these results suggest that a WGD event in durian's lineage led to the expansion and diversification of pathways related to durian volatiles, such as those involved in sulfur processing (including <i>MGL</i>), lipid volatiles, and ethylene. Upregulation of these genes in durian may be linked to increased VSC production, thereby contributing to durian odor.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Potential association between VSC production and ripening</h3><p>In climacteric plant species, ethylene sensing has been shown to act as a major trigger of fruit ripening. Ethylene production is a three-step reaction, beginning with transfer of the adenosyl group in ATP to <span class="u-small-caps">L</span>-methionine by SAM synthase, followed by conversion of <span class="u-small-caps">L</span>-methionine to 1-aminocyclopropane-1-carboxylic acid and ethylene by the enzymes ACS and ACO, respectively<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Rodrigues, M.A., Bianchetti, R.E. &amp; Freschi, L. Shedding light on ethylene metabolism in higher plants. Front. Plant Sci. 5, 665 (2014)." href="/articles/ng.3972#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d33465e1940">40</a></sup>. Our genomic and transcriptomic findings suggest a potential association between durian odor and fruit ripening, based on the central role of methionine in both processes. In this model (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3972#Fig5">Fig. 5a</a>), methionine acts as a precursor amino acid for both VSCs and ethylene via MGL and ACS activity, respectively, and both <i>MGL</i> and <i>ACS</i> are upregulated in durian fruits (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3972#Fig4">Figs. 4d</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3972#Fig5">5b</a>). We also observed upregulation of other genes plausibly related to durian ripening, including <i>PME40</i>, which encodes a pectinesterase that has an important role in fruit softening in strawberries<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Castillejo, C., de la Fuente, J.I., Iannetta, P., Botella, M.Ã. &amp; Valpuesta, V. Pectin esterase gene family in strawberry fruit: study of FaPE1, a ripening-specific isoform. J. Exp. Bot. 55, 909â918 (2004)." href="/articles/ng.3972#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d33465e1963">41</a></sup>; <i>C71BV</i>, which encodes a cytochrome P450 upregulated in banana fruits<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Asif, M.H. et al. Transcriptome analysis of ripe and unripe fruit tissue of banana identifies major metabolic networks involved in fruit ripening process. BMC Plant Biol. 14, 316 (2014)." href="/articles/ng.3972#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d33465e1971">42</a></sup>; and <i>BXL1</i>, which encodes a Î²-<span class="u-small-caps">D</span>-xylosidase involved in cell wall modification of ripening tomatoes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Itai, A., Ishihara, K. &amp; Bewley, J.D. Characterization of expression, and cloning, of Î²-D-xylosidase and Î±-l-arabinofuranosidase in developing and ripening tomato (Lycopersicon esculentum Mill.) fruit. J. Exp. Bot. 54, 2615â2622 (2003)." href="/articles/ng.3972#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d33465e1981">43</a></sup>. To study associations between <i>MGL</i>, <i>ACS</i>, VSCs, and ethylene, we profiled six durian fruits at different ripening stages (six arils for each stage): 1 month post-anthesis, 2 months post-anthesis, 2.5 months post-anthesis (ripe), and over 3 d post-abscission (fully ripe). qRTâPCR analysis of <i>MGLa</i> and <i>MGLb</i> in durian arils over the post-anthesis period demonstrated a coordinated spike in expression over the ripening period, in concert with increasing VSC levels as detected by profiling with headspace gas chromatography coupled with mass spectrometry (GCâMS) and increasing ethylene levels (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3972#Fig5">Fig. 5c</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3972#MOESM3">Supplementary Tables 11â13</a>). The availability of global mass spectrometry profiles also allowed us to identify different types of VSCs such as sulfide complexes and disulfide analogs (details of different VSCs are provided in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3972#MOESM3">Supplementary Table 11</a>), the most abundant of which was diethyl disulfide (DEDS), a compound produced through redox reactions from organic thiols. The levels of <i>MGL</i> expression, DEDS, and ethylene remained high for post-abscission fruits maintained over 3 d at room temperature (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3972#Fig5">Fig. 5d</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="The Yang cycle may link ethylene production and volatile sulfur compound production."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 5: The Yang cycle may link ethylene production and volatile sulfur compound production.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.3972/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.3972/MediaObjects/41588_2017_Article_BFng3972_Fig5_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.3972/MediaObjects/41588_2017_Article_BFng3972_Fig5_HTML.jpg" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" width="685" height="537"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) The Yang cycle (black arrows) salvages methionine consumed for ethylene production (pink), while sulfur-related processes (green) replenish and consume the sulfur moiety from methionine. (<b>b</b>) Upregulation of ripening- and sulfur-related genes in durian fruit. (<b>c</b>) MS analysis of the VSC DEDS, qRTâPCR analysis of <i>MGL</i>, and measurement of ethylene across durian fruits at three ripening stages (1 month, 2 months, and 2.5 months post-anthesis) show a coordinated spike toward later ripening stages. Dots correspond to individual samples, and bars represent means Â± s.e.m. (<b>d</b>) Increased levels of DEDS, <i>MGL</i>, and ethylene are maintained in post-abscission fruits (fully ripened) over 3 d at room temperature. Dots correspond to individual samples, and bars represent means Â± s.e.m.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.3972/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The usage of methionine as a precursor for both odor (VSCs) and ripening (ethylene) in durian suggests the presence of a rapidly sulfur-depleted environment in durian fruits, as (i) sulfur is a scarce nutrient, (ii) VSC production via MGL is a sulfur sink, and (iii) 5-methylthioadenosine (MTA), the byproduct of ACS, contains a reduced sulfur group. Consistent with a low-sulfur state, we observed upregulation in fruits of several genes related to sulfur sensing and scavenging, including <i>SDI1</i>, a sulfur-sensing gene, and upregulation of <i>BGL</i>, which encodes a Î²-glucosidase that cleaves sulfur from glucosinolate<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Zheng, Z.-L., Zhang, B. &amp; Leustek, T. Transceptors at the boundary of nutrient transporters and receptors: a new role for Arabidopsis SULTR1;2 in sulfur sensing. Front. Plant Sci. 5, 710 (2014)." href="/articles/ng.3972#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d33465e2062">44</a></sup>. An additional pathway for salvaging sulfur from MTA is the Yang cycle, which allows sulfur moieties from MTA to be recycled back to methionine<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Baur, A.H. &amp; Yang, S.F. Methionine metabolism in apple tissue in relation to ethylene biosynthesis. Phytochemistry 11, 3207â3214 (1972)." href="/articles/ng.3972#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d33465e2066">45</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Miyazaki, J.H. &amp; Yang, S.F. Metabolism of 5-methylthioribose to methionine. Plant Physiol. 84, 277â281 (1987)." href="/articles/ng.3972#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d33465e2069">46</a></sup>. In summary, our results suggest that the complex aroma of durian is possibly linked to durian fruit ripening, as both ethylene and VSC production are connected to the same regulatory processes and metabolites. However, we emphasize that, in the current study, the potential associations between <i>MGL</i> expression, VSC generation, and ethylene production are correlative and that further functional experiments will be required to establish a direct causal link between these molecular entities.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec8" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec8-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec8">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec8-content"><p>Although durian is well known as a delicacy in tropical food, research focused on it has been hampered by an absence of genetic resources. The <i>D. zibethinus</i> Musang King draft genome thus provides a useful resource for durian agronomy and is, to our knowledge, the first and most complete genome assembly of durian. The <i>D. zibethinus</i> assembly also represents the first from the Helicteroideae subfamily and is thus also valuable for evolutionary phylogenomic studies, similar to cotton and cacao, whose genomes are available and for which genetic research is actively pursued.</p><p>Ancient polyploidization events are important evolutionary drivers for the emergence of new phenotypes and new species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Otto, S.P. The evolutionary consequences of polyploidy. Cell 131, 452â462 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.3972#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d33465e2094">22</a></sup>. Once duplication occurs, novel or specialized functions can arise in redundant alleles, and new regulatory mechanisms can develop through genomic rearrangements. Our observation that durian likely shares the previously described cotton WGD has two implications. First, other subfamilies within Malvaceae, after divergence from the Byttnerioideae subfamily (including cacao), are also likely to have the cotton-specific WGD. Second, this WGD event, which has been proposed to have driven the evolution of unique <i>Gossypium</i> traits<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Paterson, A.H. et al. Repeated polyploidization of Gossypium genomes and the evolution of spinnable cotton fibres. Nature 492, 423â427 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.3972#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d33465e2101">26</a></sup>, may also have been involved in driving the evolution of unique durian traits, as well as the radiation of different Malvaceae subfamilies around the same time<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Salman-Minkov, A., Sabath, N. &amp; Mayrose, I. Whole-genome duplication as a key factor in crop domestication. Nature Plants 2, 16115 (2016)." href="/articles/ng.3972#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d33465e2105">47</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Soltis, P.S. &amp; Soltis, D.E. Ancient WGD events as drivers of key innovations in angiosperms. Curr. Opin. Plant Biol. 30, 159â165 (2016)." href="/articles/ng.3972#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d33465e2108">48</a></sup>.</p><p>Our current study focused on analyzing biological processes related to durian odor. VSCs have been identified as major contributors to durian smell, and we observed upregulation of sulfur-related pathways in durian fruit arils in comparison to other durian plant organs and fruits from other species. At the genomic level, we also identified expansions in gene families in sulfur-related pathways, most notably in the <i>MGL</i> gene, whose product has been shown to catalyze the breakdown of methionine into VSCs in microbes and plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="RÃ©beillÃ©, F. et al. Methionine catabolism in Arabidopsis cells is initiated by a Î³-cleavage process and leads to S-methylcysteine and isoleucine syntheses. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 103, 15687â15692 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.3972#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d33465e2118">38</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Gonda, I. et al. Catabolism of l-methionine in the formation of sulfur and other volatiles in melon (Cucumis melo L.) fruit. Plant J. 74, 458â472 (2013)." href="/articles/ng.3972#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d33465e2121">39</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Kudou, D. et al. Structure of the antitumour enzyme l-methionine Î³-lyase from Pseudomonas putida at 1.8 Ã resolution. J. Biochem. 141, 535â544 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.3972#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d33465e2124">49</a></sup>. More specifically, our analysis suggests a distinct role for <i>MGLb</i> genes in the durian fruit, where these genes were found to be significantly upregulated. MGL functions as a homotetramer in which each subunit requires a pyridoxal 5â²-phosphate (PLP) cofactor, with the active sites located in the vicinity of PLP and the substrate-binding site<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Gonda, I. et al. Catabolism of l-methionine in the formation of sulfur and other volatiles in melon (Cucumis melo L.) fruit. Plant J. 74, 458â472 (2013)." href="/articles/ng.3972#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d33465e2131">39</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Kudou, D. et al. Structure of the antitumour enzyme l-methionine Î³-lyase from Pseudomonas putida at 1.8 Ã resolution. J. Biochem. 141, 535â544 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.3972#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d33465e2134">49</a></sup>. While these active sites are conserved in both durian MGLa and MGLb protein sequences as compared to other plant and bacterial MGL sequences, regions proximal to the active sites, which are conserved in other plant species, exhibited more mutations in <i>MGLb</i> than in <i>MGLa</i> (four versus two, respectively; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3972#Fig10">Supplementary Fig. 5</a>). The impact of these mutations on the function of durian <i>MGL</i> gene products requires further investigation.</p><p>Our analysis also suggests a potential association between <i>MGL</i> and <i>ACS</i> in the durian fruit, whose coordination may involve the Yang cycle.
