Previous durian studies have also noted correlations between VSC production and increased ethylene production<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Maninang, J.S., Wongs-Aree, C., Kanlayanarat, S., Sugaya, S. &amp; Gemma, H. Influence of maturity and postharvest treatment on the volatile profile and physiological properties of the durian (Durio zibethinus Murray) fruit. Int. Food Res. J. 18, 1067â1075 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.3972#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d33465e2160">36</a></sup>; however, a genetic link behind these observations has not been demonstrated. It is possible that linking odor and ripening may provide an evolutionary advantage for durian in facilitating fruit dispersal. Certain plants whose primary dispersal vectors are primates with more advanced olfactory systems show a shift in odor at ripening<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Hodgkison, R. et al. Fruit bats and bat fruits: the evolution of fruit scent in relation to the foraging behaviour of bats in the New and Old World tropics. Funct. Ecol. 27, 1075â1084 (2013)." href="/articles/ng.3972#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d33465e2164">50</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Borges, R.M., BessiÃ¨re, J.M. &amp; Hossaert-McKey, M. The chemical ecology of seed dispersal in monoecious and dioecious figs. Funct. Ecol. 22, 484â493 (2008)." href="/articles/ng.3972#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d33465e2167">51</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="LomÃ¡scolo, S.B., Levey, D.J., Kimball, R.T., Bolker, B.M. &amp; Alborn, H.T. Dispersers shape fruit diversity in Ficus (Moraceae). Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 107, 14668â14672 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.3972#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d33465e2170">52</a></sup>. Similarly, durianâby emanating an extremely pungent odor at ripeningâappears to have the characteristic of a plant whose main dispersal vectors are odor-enticed primates rather than visually enticed animals.</p><p>The durian genome assembly creates a large scope for further studies. As an example, rapid commercialization of durian has led to the proliferation of cultivars with a wide discrepancy in prices and little way to verify the authenticity of the fruit products at scale. A high-quality genome assembly may aid in identifying cultivar-specific sequences, including SNPs related to important cultivar-specific traits (such as taste, texture, and odor), and allow molecular barcoding of different durian cultivars for rapid quality control. At a more basic level, such genetic information is vital to better understanding of durian biodiversity. The <i>Durio</i> genus comprises more than 30 known species, some of which do not produce edible fruit (for example, <i>Durio singaporensis</i>) and others that are outcompeted in nature and face extinction. Further studies will help to elucidate the ecological roles of these important and fascinating tropical plants.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec9" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec9-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec9">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec9-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec10">Sample collection.</h3><p>Durian samples used for genomic analysis (Musang King fruit stalk) and transcriptomic analysis (three Musang King fruit arils, three Monthong fruit arils, and three Puang Manee fruit arils, as well as three vegetative parts (stem, root, and leaf)) were from a plantation in the Bentong region of Pahang, West Malaysia, while durian fruits used for validation studies (MS profiling of VSCs, qRTâPCR of <i>MGL</i>, and measurement of ethylene) were from a separate plantation in Sendenak, Johor, West Malaysia, at â1Â° 41â² 55.8â³ N, 103Â° 28â² 57.7â³ E. For the different ripening stages, we sourced fruiting trees in this plantation in July 2016 and isolated three trees of ages 28, 45, and 45 years. Two durian fruits were collected per tree in each ripening stage: 1 month post-anthesis, 2 months post-anthesis, 2.5 months post-anthesis, and post-abscission. In each ripening stage (1 month post-anthesis, 2 months post-anthesis, 2.5 months post-anthesis, and post-abscission), we profiled six arils from six durian fruits for qRTâPCR and MS and profiled ethylene for six durian whole fruits. For ethylene measurements, samples that did not obtain stable profiles were discarded.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec11">DNA extraction and library preparation.</h3><p>Genomic DNA was extracted from a fruit stalk using Plant DNAzol reagent (Life Technologies) following the manufacturer's recommendations.</p><p>SMRTbell DNA library preparation and sequencing with P6-C4 chemistry were performed in accordance with the manufacturer's protocols (Pacific Biosciences). 50 Î¼g of high-quality genomic DNA was used to generate a 20-kb SMRTbell library using a lower end of 10 kb in size selection on the BluePippin (Sage Science). Initial titration runs were performed to optimize SMRT cell loading and yield. The genome was sequenced employing 52 SMRT cells on the PacBio RSII platform (Pacific Biosciences) by sequencing provider Icahn Mount Sinai School of Medicine.</p><p>A short-read genomic library was prepared using the TruSeq Nano DNA Library Preparation kit (Illumina) in accordance with the manufacturer's recommendations. Briefly, 1 Î¼g of extracted intact genomic DNA was sheared (Covaris), with an insert size of 200â300 bp, and ligated to adaptors. DNA fragments were subjected to PCR, and the DNA library was used in paired-end sequencing (2 Ã 100 bp, <span class="stix">â¼</span>750 million paired-end reads, <span class="stix">â¼</span>202Ã coverage) on the Illumina HiSeq 2500 sequencer.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">RNA extraction and library preparation.</h3><p>RNA-seq experiments were conducted for Musang King fruit arils (three arils, 51.6 million paired-end reads each on average) and a combined non-fruit group comprising stem (55.6 million reads), leaf (56.9 million reads), and root (53.2 million reads) profiles. We also sequenced fruit arils of Monthong and Puang Manee cultivars (three arils each, 90 million reads each on average). Our sample sizes and read depths enabled significance testing of differentially expressed genes and gene sets<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Ching, T., Huang, S. &amp; Garmire, L.X. Power analysis and sample size estimation for RNA-Seq differential expression. RNA 20, 1684â1696 (2014)." href="/articles/ng.3972#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d33465e2221">53</a></sup> (by DESeq2 and GSEA).</p><p>RNA was extracted using the PureLink RNA mini kit (Life Technologies) following the manufacturer's recommended protocol.</p><p>For the construction of RNA-seq libraries, 2 Î¼g of total RNA was processed using the TruSeq RNA Sample Preparation kit (Illumina) followed by sequencing on the Illumina HiSeq 2500 platform.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">Genome assembly.</h3><p>The main assembly was performed on full PacBio long reads using two diploid assembly approaches. CANU<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Koren, S. et al. Canu: scalable and accurate long-read assembly via adaptive k-mer weighting and repeat separation. Genome Res. 27, 722â736 (2017)." href="/articles/ng.3972#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d33465e2239">54</a></sup> was run with both default and high-sensitivity settings, and FALCON was run with length_cutoff = 5000 for initial mapping of seed reads for the error-correction phase. The CANU assembly had a N50 value indicating a less contiguous assembly, and the assembly did not substantially improve even after applying Redundans, a tool that detects redundant heterozygous contigs and attempts to reduce assembly fragmentation. We subsequently used FALCON because it was better able to phase the diploid genome as well as indicating better contiguity from N50 evaluations. Errors in the PacBio reads were corrected within the FALCON pipeline. Screening for contamination was handled with PacBio's whitelisting pipeline. We subsequently compared a number of length_cutoff values in FALCON for the mapping of seed reads for preassembly ranging from 2,000 to 11,000. On the basis of the contig N50 results, we chose length_cutoff = 9,500 for the preassembly step. We additionally configured DBsplit (-x500, --s400), daligner (-B128, -l4800, --s100, -k18, -h480), and overlap_filtering (--max_diff 100, --max_cov 100) with parameters optimized for our plant assembly. The results from this step were used to perform phased diploid genome assembly using FALCON-Unzip (default parameters). Subsequently, the draft assembly was polished using Arrow (see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="section anchor" href="/articles/ng.3972#Sec29">URLs</a>) over three iterations and finally corrected using Illumina short reads with Pilon.</p><p>For additional details about genome assembly validation, see the <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3972#MOESM1">Supplementary Note</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14">Estimation of genome size and ploidy.</h3><p>We estimated genome size by flow cytometry<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Marie, D. &amp; Brown, S.C. A cytometric exercise in plant DNA histograms, with 2C values for 70 species. Biol. Cell 78, 41â51 (1993)." href="/articles/ng.3972#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d33465e2261">55</a></sup> and obtained an estimate of 800 Mb (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3972#MOESM3">Supplementary Table 2</a>). We additionally estimated genome size by <i>k</i>-mer distribution analysis with the program Jellyfish<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="MarÃ§ais, G. &amp; Kingsford, C. A fast, lock-free approach for efficient parallel counting of occurrences of k-mers. Bioinformatics 27, 764â770 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.3972#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d33465e2271">56</a></sup> (<i>k</i> = 31), using Illumina short reads, and obtained an estimate of 738 Mb. For additional details about ploidy estimation, see the <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3972#MOESM1">Supplementary Note</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec15">CHiCAGO and Hi-C library preparation and sequencing.</h3><p><span class="stix">â¼</span>500 ng of high-molecular-weight genomic DNA (mean fragment length = 150 kb) was reconstituted into chromatin <i>in vitro</i> for the ChiCAGO library. Chromatin was fixed with formaldehyde for the CHiCAGO library. For the Hi-C library, chromatin was fixed in place with formaldehyde in the nucleus. Fixed chromatin was digested with DpnII, 5â² overhangs filled in with biotinylated nucleotides, and free blunt ends were ligated. After ligation, cross-links were reversed and the DNA was purified from protein. Purified DNA was treated to remove biotin that was not internal to the ligated fragments. The DNA was then sheared to a mean fragment size of <span class="stix">â¼</span>350 bp, and sequencing libraries were generated using NEBNext Ultra enzymes and Illumina-compatible adaptors. Biotin-containing fragments were isolated using streptavidin beads before PCR enrichment of each library. The libraries were sequenced on an Illumina HiSeq X to produce 233 million (CHiCAGO) and 280 million (Hi-C) 2 Ã 150-bp paired-end reads, which provided 379.52Ã (1- to 100-kb pairs) and 4,370.55Ã (100- to 1,000-kb pairs) physical coverage of the genome, respectively.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec16">Scaffolding with HiRise.</h3><p>The input <i>de novo</i> assembly, shotgun reads, CHiCAGO library reads, and Dovetail Hi-C library reads were used as input data for HiRise, a software pipeline designed specifically to use proximity-ligation data to scaffold genome assemblies. An iterative analysis was conducted. First, shotgun and CHiCAGO library sequences were aligned to the draft input assembly using a modified SNAP read mapper<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Zaharia, M. et al. Faster and more accurate sequence alignment with SNAP. Preprint at 
                    https://arxiv.org/abs/1111.5572
                    
                   (2011)." href="/articles/ng.3972#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d33465e2304">57</a></sup>. The separation between CHiCAGO read pairs mapping within draft scaffolds was analyzed by HiRise to produce a likelihood model for the genomic distance between read pairs, and the model was used to identify and break putative misjoins, to score prospective joins, and to make joins above a selected threshold. After aligning and scaffolding CHiCAGO data, Dovetail Hi-C library sequences were aligned and scaffolded following the same method. After scaffolding, shotgun sequences were used to close gaps between contigs. Scaffolding with CHiCAGO libraries made 167 breaks and 2,359 joins to the contig assembly. We additionally used Hi-C libraries to detect 7 breaks and 706 joins.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec17">Transcriptome assembly.</h3><p>RNA-seq reads were preprocessed by trim_galore to remove contaminating sequences from adaptors as well as sequences with low base quality. Reads were then aligned to the reference genome using HiSat<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Kim, D., Langmead, B. &amp; Salzberg, S.L. HISAT: a fast spliced aligner with low memory requirements. Nat. Methods 12, 357â360 (2015)." href="/articles/ng.3972#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d33465e2316">58</a></sup>. Reference-guided assembly was performed on the resulting alignment to produce a plant-organ-specific set of transcripts using StringTie<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Pertea, M. et al. StringTie enables improved reconstruction of a transcriptome from RNA-seq reads. Nat. Biotechnol. 33, 290â295 (2015)." href="/articles/ng.3972#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d33465e2320">59</a></sup>. A non-redundant set of transcripts observed in all samples assembled was merged using the merge functionality in StringTie.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec18">Genome annotation.</h3><p>We generated a <i>de novo</i> repeat library from our assembly using RepeatModeler. We removed <i>de novo</i> repeats that matched known <i>Arabidopsis</i> genes (BLASTN <i>E</i> &lt; 10<sup>â5</sup>). We annotated our <i>de novo</i> repeats using the RepeatClassifier module of RepeatModeler. We combined our <i>de novo</i> library with the RepBase plant repeat database<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Bao, W., Kojima, K.K. &amp; Kohany, O. Repbase Update, a database of repetitive elements in eukaryotic genomes. Mob. DNA 6, 11 (2015)." href="/articles/ng.3972#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d33465e2353">60</a></sup> and ran RepeatMasker on the assembly (default parameters).</p><p>We used the Maker genome annotation pipeline for gene prediction. Gene evidence was provided in the form of 151,594 protein sequences from four plant species (<i>Arabidopsis</i>, grape, rice, and soybean) and 178,840 transcripts assembled from our RNA-seq data. For details on how Maker was run, see the <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3972#MOESM1">Supplementary Note</a>.</p><p>We transferred GO annotations to our predicted genes using Blast2GO, incorporating sequence homology with <i>Arabidopsis</i> genes using BLASTP (best hit with <i>E</i> &lt; 10<sup>â5</sup>) and functional domain search with InterProScan<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Jones, P. et al. InterProScan 5: genome-scale protein function classification. Bioinformatics 30, 1236â1240 (2014)." href="/articles/ng.3972#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d33465e2377">61</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec19">Short-read alignment and SNP calling.</h3><p>Previously preprocessed Illumina short reads were used for paired-end remapping to the reference assembly using BWA-MEM<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Li, H. Aligning sequence reads, clone sequences and assembly contigs with BWA-MEM. Preprint at 
                    https://arxiv.org/abs/1303.3997
                    
                   (2013)." href="/articles/ng.3972#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d33465e2389">62</a></sup>. The resulting alignments were sorted using SAMtools<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Li, H. et al. The Sequence Alignment/Map format and SAMtools. Bioinformatics 25, 2078â2079 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.3972#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d33465e2393">63</a></sup> and PCR, and optical duplicates were marked and removed using Picardtools (see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="section anchor" href="/articles/ng.3972#Sec29">URLs</a>). The processed alignments were then used to infer SNPs with FreeBayes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Garrison, E. &amp; Marth, G. Haplotype-based variant detection from short-read sequencing. Preprint at 
                    https://arxiv.org/abs/1207.3907/
                    
                   (2012)." href="/articles/ng.3972#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d33465e2400">64</a></sup> using parameters -C 5, -m 20 --F 0.01. Resulting SNP calls were further filtered to remove those with quality less than 20.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec20">Molecular phylogenetic analysis.</h3><p>We performed gene family clustering for 11 plant species using OrthoMCL (default parameters) on protein sequences. Molecular phylogenetic analysis was performed using a strict set of 47 single-copy orthologous genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3972#MOESM3">Supplementary Table 5</a>), derived by intersecting 344 single-copy gene families found by OrthoMCL with 395 single-copy gene families published. We codon aligned each gene family with MUSCLE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Edgar, R.C. MUSCLE: multiple sequence alignment with high accuracy and high throughput. Nucleic Acids Res. 32, 1792â1797 (2004)." href="/articles/ng.3972#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d33465e2416">65</a></sup> and curated the alignments with Gblocks<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Castresana, J. Selection of conserved blocks from multiple alignments for their use in phylogenetic analysis. Mol. Biol. Evol. 17, 540â552 (2000)." href="/articles/ng.3972#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d33465e2420">66</a></sup>. Phylogeny analysis was performed with BEAST2. For details about model selection for phylogeny, see the <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3972#MOESM1">Supplementary Note</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec21">Whole-genome duplication analysis.</h3><p>We used MCScan<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Tang, H. et al. Synteny and collinearity in plant genomes. Science 320, 486â488 (2008)." href="/articles/ng.3972#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d33465e2435">67</a></sup> and the CoGe Comparative Genomics Platform<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Lyons, E. &amp; Freeling, M. How to usefully compare homologous plant genes and chromosomes as DNA sequences. Plant J. 53, 661â673 (2008)." href="/articles/ng.3972#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d33465e2439">68</a></sup> to detect syntenic blocks (regions with at least five collinear genes) and calculate <i>K</i><sub>s</sub> rates for syntenic genes. To analyze the durian and <i>G. raimondii</i> WGDs, we first identified paralogous durian and <i>G. raimondii</i> gene pairs originating from their respective WGDs. In durian and <i>G. raimondii</i> separately, we modeled the distribution of <i>K</i><sub>s</sub> rates as a mixture model and identified syntenic gene pairs falling within the first peak Â± 1 s.d. as paralogs likely derived from the most recent WGD event. Next, we identified OrthoMCL gene families consisting of one such paralogous durian pair, one such paralogous <i>G. raimondii</i> pair, and one gene each from cacao and <i>Arabidopsis</i>. In each of these 62 families, we matched each durian paralog with its cotton ortholog (among the two cotton paralogs), by setting the durianâcotton pair with the lowest synonymous distance as orthologs (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3972#MOESM3">Supplementary Table 7</a>). We then used BEAST2 to estimate the phylogenetic tree and divergence times of the paralogous and orthologous genes in durian and <i>G. raimondii</i>, along with cacao and <i>Arabidopsis</i>. BEAST2 was run as described above.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec22">Transcriptome analysis.</h3><p>Previously aligned RNA-seq data were counted against the predicted gene models using HTSeq-count<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 69" title="Anders, S., Pyl, P.T. &amp; Huber, W. HTSeqâa Python framework to work with high-throughput sequencing data. Bioinformatics 31, 166â169 (2015)." href="/articles/ng.3972#ref-CR69" id="ref-link-section-d33465e2485">69</a></sup>. Raw counts were fitted on a negative binomial distribution, and differential expression was tested for using DESeq2. Hidden variables were removed using the sva package and added to the study design before running DESeq2. Genes were sorted according to their log<sub>2</sub>-transformed fold-change values after shrinkage in DESeq2 and used for gene set analysis with fgsea's implementation of GSEA. Significant gene sets were used to perform leading-edge analysis. We detected clusters of pathways that shared many leading-edge genes using a community detection algorithm and manually curated these clusters to elucidate the important phenotype-associated pathway groups visualized on the bubble plots. Leading edges that also had a fold change of 2 were used to generate bubble plots. DESeq normalized gene expression was used in all bubble plots and heat maps. For additional details about transcriptomic comparisons against other plant species, see the <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3972#MOESM1">Supplementary Note</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec23">Gene family expansion analysis.</h3><p>For gene family expansion analysis, we considered only six plants from the OrthoMCL gene family results: durian, two cotton species (<i>G. raimondii</i> and <i>G. arboretum</i>), cacao, <i>Arabidopsis</i>, and rice. We considered a gene family expanded in durian if (i) the number of durian gene members was greater than or equal to the number of gene members for every other plant considered, (ii) the number of durian gene members was greater than the number of gene members for cacao, <i>Arabidopsis</i>, and rice, and (iii) the number of gene members for all plants considered was greater than 0. These criteria were chosen to detect gene family expansions in durian, as well as expansions in both durian and the closely related cotton species. At the pathway level, we considered whether each pathway was expanded in durian by checking if its durian gene members were enriched in belonging to expanded gene families (Fisher's exact test with BenjaminiâHochberg multiple-hypothesis correction).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec24">Quantitative real-time PCR.</h3><p>cDNA synthesis was performed on 200 ng of total RNA with the SensiFAST cDNA Synthesis kit (Bioline) followed by preamplification with SsoAdvanced PreAmp Supermix (Bio-Rad) according to the manufacturer's recommendations. The cDNA was diluted tenfold, and a 1-Î¼l aliquot of cDNA was used for each qRTâPCR reaction using SsoAdvanced SYBR Green Supermix (Bio-Rad). All samples were run in triplicate for each primer combination. Thermal cycling was performed on a CFX C1000 System (Bio-Rad) in 96-well plates with an initiation step at 95 Â°C for 30 s, 40 cycles of denaturation at 95 Â°C for 5 s and extension at 60 Â°C for 15 s, and a final melting curve analysis on each reaction to confirm the specificity of amplification. Relative quantification of gene expression was performed using three durian housekeeping genes, <i>CAC</i>, <i>DNAJ</i>, and <i>APT</i>, as reference. Ct values were determined using CFXTM manager software (Bio-Rad) and exported into MS Excel (Microsoft) for statistical analysis. Real-time efficiencies (<i>E</i>) were calculated from the slopes of standard curves for each gene (<i>E</i> = 10(1/slope)).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec25">Mass spectrometry.</h3><p>Headspace solid-phase microextraction (HS-SPME) was used to identify sulfur-containing volatiles in durian fruits. Harvested fruits were kept in a room maintained at 25 Â°C with a relative humidity of 80â90%. 500 mg of each sample aril was ground under liquid nitrogen and transferred into a 20-ml brown glass headspace vial (Restek). 50 nmol of the internal standard, thiophene (Sigma-Aldrich), was added to each sample vial, and vials were immediately locked with air-tight headspace screw caps. An 85-Î¼m carboxen on polymethylsiloxane (CAR/PDMS) (Supelco, Bellefonte) was used as the SPME. The SPME fiber was conditioned according to the manufacturer's instructions. Online headspace sampling, extraction, and GCâMS analysis were performed on the Agilent 7200 GC-QTOF mass spectrometer equipped with a PAL CTC Headspace autosampler. Headspace extraction and desorption were carried out accordingly. Each sample was incubated for 30 min at 40 Â°C, followed by extraction of the volatiles for 30 min at 40 Â°C and 250 r.p.m. Conditioning was performed for 5 min at 250 Â°C followed by injection into the GCâMS instrument for 1 min at 230 Â°C. The injector was operated in a 'split' mode at a ratio of 50:1, and the flow rate was maintained at 1 ml/min. An Agilent 19091S-433HP-5MS 5% Phenyl Methyl Silox (30 m Ã 0.25 mm Ã 0.25 Î¼m) column was used to separate the volatiles. The oven temperature was increased from 40 Â°C to 200 Â°C at a rate of 5 Â°C/min followed by further increase to 280 Â°C at a rate of 40 Â°C/min for 5 min. The total run time was 39 min. Other parameters are as follows: ion source, EI; source temperature, 230 Â°C; electron energy, 70 eV; acquisition rate, 2 spectra/s; acquisition time, 500 ms/spectrum; mass range, 40â250 a.m.u. Mass spectra were analyzed using Agilent MassHunter Qualitative Analysis software version B.05.00. Identification of metabolites was performed using the NIST version 11 library based on spectral matching. Quantifications of sulfur-containing metabolites are expressed as follows: peak area of metabolite/peak area of internal standard/0.5 g = abundance/gram.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec26">Ethylene gas measurement.</h3><p>Harvested fruits were all kept in a room maintained at 25 Â°C with a relative humidity of 80â90%. All daily samplings for the post-abscission fruits were conducted at the same starting time to ensure consistency. Each durian fruit was placed in an airtight 8-L container fitted with gas-sampling ports connected to the ethylene gas analyzer (model F-900, based on electrochemical sensors; Felix Instruments). After 60 min, the headspace air was sampled and measured under polarcept settings following the manufacturer's recommendations. Ethylene measurements were obtained as a mean of three readings sampled at 10-min intervals.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec27">Statistics.</h3><p>To determine differentially expressed genes, we used DESeq2 to model raw gene counts for gene expression on a negative binomial model. <i>P</i> values were obtained from a two-tailed test with BenjaminiâHochberg multiple-hypothesis correction. To determine disregulated pathways based on gene expression, we used GSEA, which applies a KolmogorovâSmirnov-like statistic. <i>P</i> values were derived from a permutation test to generate empirical null distributions. To determine significant pathways with gene family expansion, we used a two-tailed Fisher's exact test with BenjaminiâHochberg multiple-hypothesis correction. All <i>P</i> values were considered significant if below a threshold of 0.05.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec28">Data availability.</h3><p>All custom scripts have been made available at <a href="https://github.com/chernycherny/FSFix">https://github.com/chernycherny/FSFix</a>. Raw reads and genome assembly have been deposited as a BioProject under accession <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/?term=PRJNA400310">PRJNA400310</a>. A <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3972#MOESM2">Life Sciences Reporting Summary</a> is available.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec29">URLs.</h3><p>Arrow, <a href="https://github.com/PacificBiosciences/GenomicConsensus">https://github.com/PacificBiosciences/GenomicConsensus</a>; Picardtools, <a href="http://broadinstitute.github.io/picard/">http://broadinstitute.github.io/picard/</a>; UN Trade Statistics, <a href="http://unstats.un.org/">http://unstats.un.org/</a>; RepeatMasker and RepeatModeler, <a href="http://www.repeatmasker.org/">http://www.repeatmasker.org/</a>.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec30" data-title="Additional information"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec30-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec30">Additional information</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec30-content"><p><b>Publisher's note:</b> Springer Nature remains neutral with regard to jurisdictional claims in published maps and institutional affiliations.</p></div></div></section>

              <p class="c-article-further-reading__journal-title" id="journal-name-3">
                <i>Bioscience Reports</i>
                (2020)
              </p>
            </li>
